template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div architecture">Hindu temple architecture</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Akshardham_angled.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Akshardham_angled.jpg/200px-Akshardham_angled.jpg" width="200" height="113" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Akshardham_angled.jpg/300px-Akshardham_angled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Akshardham_angled.jpg/400px-Akshardham_angled.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4128" data-file-height="2322" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Akshardham_(Delhi)" title="Akshardham (Delhi)">Akshardham Temple in Delhi</a> is one of the largest Hindu temple in the world.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg/200px-BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg/300px-BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg/400px-BAPS_Robbinsville_Mandir_-_mandir_interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Once completed, the <a href="/wiki/Akshardham_(New_Jersey)" title="Akshardham (New Jersey)">Swaminarayanan Akshardham Temple Complex</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> will be one of the largest temple complexes outside India.<sup id="cite_ref-LargestHinduTempleNJ_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LargestHinduTempleNJ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div></div>\n<p>A  <b>Hindu temple</b> is a symbolic house, seat and body of god. It is a structure designed to bring human beings and gods together, using symbolism to express the ideas and beliefs of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michell1977p61_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michell1977p61-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The symbolism and structure of a Hindu temple are rooted in Vedic traditions, deploying circles and squares.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A temple incorporates all elements of Hindu cosmos\xe2\x80\x94presenting the good, the evil and the human, as well as the elements of Hindu sense of cyclic time and the essence of life\xe2\x80\x94symbolically presenting <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kama" title="Kama">kama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moksa" class="mw-redirect" title="Moksa">moksa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spiritual principles symbolically represented in Hindu temples are given in the ancient Sanskrit texts of India (for example, <a href="/wiki/Veda" class="mw-redirect" title="Veda">Vedas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Upanishad" class="mw-redirect" title="Upanishad">Upanishads</a>), while their structural rules are described in various ancient Sanskrit treatises on architecture (Brhat Samhita, Vastu Sastras).<sup id="cite_ref-susanlchap4_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susanlchap4-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The layout, the motifs, the plan and the building process recite ancient rituals, geometric symbolisms, and reflect beliefs and values innate within various schools of Hinduism.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A Hindu temple is a spiritual destination for many Hindus, as well as landmarks around which ancient arts, community celebrations and economy have flourished.<sup id="cite_ref-bstein_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bstein-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hindu temples come in many styles, are situated in diverse locations, deploy different construction methods and are adapted to different deities and regional beliefs,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> yet almost all of them share certain core ideas, symbolism and themes. They are found in South Asia particularly <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, in southeast Asian countries such as <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, and islands of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  and countries such as <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, and countries with a significant Hindu community.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The current state and outer appearance of Hindu temples reflect arts, materials and designs as they evolved over two millennia; they also reflect the effect of conflicts between Hinduism and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> since the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Akshardham_(New_Jersey)" title="Akshardham (New Jersey)">Swaminarayanan Akshardham</a> in <a href="/wiki/Robbinsville,_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Robbinsville, New Jersey">Robbinsville</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, between the <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_metropolitan_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia metropolitan area">Philadelphia</a> metropolitan areas, was inaugurated in 2014 as the world\'s largest Hindu temple.<sup id="cite_ref-LargestHinduTempleNJ_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LargestHinduTempleNJ-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Significance_and_meaning_of_a_Hindu_temple"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span <span class="toctext">The teams that built Hindu temples</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Social_functions_of_Hindu_temples"><span temples</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Historical_development_and_destruction"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Historical development and destruction</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Customs_and_etiquette"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Customs and etiquette</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Regional_variations_in_Hindu_temples"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Regional variations in Hindu temples</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#North_Indian_temples"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">North Indian temples</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Temples_in_West_Bengal"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in West Bengal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Temples_in_Odisha"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Odisha</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Temples_of_Goa_and_other_Konkani_temples"><span class="tocnumber">8.4</span> <span class="toctext">Temples of Goa and other Konkani temples</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#South_Indian_and_Sri_Lankan_temples"><span class="tocnumber">8.5</span> <span class="toctext">South Indian and Sri Lankan temples</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#Temples_in_Kerala"><span class="tocnumber">8.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Kerala</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-23"><a href="#Temples_in_Karnataka_(Tulu_Nadu)"><span class="tocnumber">8.5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Karnataka (Tulu Nadu)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-24"><a href="#Temples_in_Tamil_Nadu"><span class="tocnumber">8.5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Tamil Nadu</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Temples_in_Cambodia"><span class="tocnumber">8.6</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Cambodia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Temples_in_Nepal"><span class="tocnumber">8.7</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Nepal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Temples_in_Vietnam"><span class="tocnumber">8.8</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Vietnam</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Temples_in_Indonesia"><span class="tocnumber">8.9</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Indonesia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Temples_in_Thailand"><span class="tocnumber">8.10</span> <span class="toctext">Temples in Thailand</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Temples_outside_Asia"><span class="tocnumber">8.11</span> <span class="toctext">Temples outside Asia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Temple_management"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Temple management</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Etymology_and_nomenclature"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology and nomenclature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance_and_meaning_of_a_Hindu_temple">Significance and meaning of a Hindu temple</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Significance and meaning of a Hindu temple">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A Hindu temple reflects a synthesis of arts, the ideals of <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>, beliefs, values, and the way of life cherished under Hinduism. It is a link between man, deities, and the Universal <i>Purusa</i> in a sacred space.<sup id="cite_ref-gmichell88_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmichell88-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a href="/wiki/File:81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg/360px-81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg.png" width="360" height="360" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg/540px-81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg/720px-81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="980" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:81_grid_Parama_Sayika_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 9x9 (81) grid \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Parama Sayika\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 layout plan (above) found in large ceremonial Hindu Temples. It is one of many grids used to build Hindu temples. In this structure of symmetry, each concentric layer has significance. The outermost layer, Paisachika padas, signify aspects of Asuras and evil; while inner Devika padas signify aspects of Devas and good. In between the good and evil is the concentric layer of Manusha padas signifying human life; All these layers surround Brahma padas, which signifies creative energy and the site for temple\xe2\x80\x99s primary idol for darsana. Finally at the very center of Brahma padas is Grabhgriya (Purusa Space), signifying Universal Principle present in everything and everyone.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In ancient Indian texts, a temple is a place for <i><a href="/wiki/Tirtha_and_Kshetra" class="mw-redirect" title="Tirtha and Kshetra">Tirtha</a></i> \xe2\x80\x93 pilgrimage.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It is a sacred site whose ambience and design attempts to symbolically condense the ideal tenets of Hindu way of life.<sup id="cite_ref-gmichell88_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmichell88-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> All the cosmic elements that create and sustain life are present in a Hindu temple \xe2\x80\x93 from fire to water, from images of nature to deities, from the feminine to the masculine, from the fleeting sounds and incense smells to the eternal nothingness yet universality at the core of the temple.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Susan Lewandowski states<sup id="cite_ref-susanlchap4_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susanlchap4-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> that the underlying principle in a Hindu temple is built around the belief that all things are one, everything is connected. The pilgrim is welcomed through 64-grid or 81-grid mathematically structured spaces, a network of art, pillars with carvings and statues that display and celebrate the four important and necessary principles of human life \xe2\x80\x93 the pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Artha" title="Artha">artha</a> (prosperity, wealth), the pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Kama" title="Kama">kama</a> (pleasure, sex), the pursuit of dharma (virtues, ethical life) and the pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a> (release, self-knowledge).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> At the center of the temple, typically below and sometimes above or next to the <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>, is mere hollow space with no decoration, symbolically representing <i>Purusa</i>, the Supreme Principle, the sacred Universal, one without form, which is present everywhere, connects everything, and is the essence of everyone. A Hindu temple is meant to encourage reflection, facilitate purification of one\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and trigger the process of inner realization within the devotee.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The specific process is left to the devotee\xe2\x80\x99s school of belief. The primary deity of different Hindu temples varies to reflect this spiritual spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Hindu tradition, there is no dividing line between the <a href="/wiki/Secular" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular">secular</a> and the sacred.<sup id="cite_ref-susanlchap4_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susanlchap4-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In the same spirit, Hindu temples are not just sacred spaces, they are also secular spaces. Their meaning and purpose have extended beyond spiritual life to social rituals and daily life, offering thus a social meaning. Some temples have served as a venue to mark festivals, to celebrate arts through dance and music, to get married or commemorate marriages,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> commemorate the birth of a child, other significant life events, or mark the death of a loved one. In political and economic life, Hindu temples have served as a venue for the succession within dynasties and landmarks around which economic activity thrived.<sup id="cite_ref-Susan_Lewandowski_pp_71-73_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Susan_Lewandowski_pp_71-73-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_and_designs_of_Hindu_temples">Forms and designs of Hindu temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Forms and designs of Hindu temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple_architecture" title="Hindu temple architecture">Hindu temple architecture</a></div>\n<p>Almost all Hindu temples take two forms: a house or a palace. A house-themed temple is a simple shelter which serves as a deity\xe2\x80\x99s home. The temple is a place where the devotee visits, just like he or she would visit a friend or relative. In <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a> school of Hinduism, temples are venues for <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a>, which is a hospitality ritual, where the deity is honored, and where devotee calls upon, attends to and connects with the deity. In other schools of Hinduism, the person may simply perform jap, or meditation, or <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">yoga</a>, or introspection in his or her temple. Palace-themed temples often incorporate more elaborate and monumental architecture.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Site">Site</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Site">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The appropriate site for a temple, suggest ancient Sanskrit texts, is near water and gardens, where lotus and flowers bloom, where swans, ducks and other birds are heard, where animals rest without fear of injury or harm.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> These harmonious places were recommended in these texts with the explanation that such are the places where gods play, and thus the best site for Hindu temples.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-susanlchap4_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susanlchap4-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami,_Karnataka,_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg/250px-Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg/375px-Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg/500px-Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Close_up_view_of_Bhutanatha_temple_at_Badami,_Karnataka,_India.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hindu temple sites cover a wide range. The most common sites are those near water bodies, embedded in nature, such as the above at Badami, Karnataka.</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>The gods always play where lakes are,<br />\nwhere the sun\xe2\x80\x99s rays are warded off by umbrellas of lotus leaf clusters,<br />\nand where clear waterpaths are made by swans<br />\nwhose breasts toss the white lotus hither and thither,<br />\nwhere swans, ducks, curleys and paddy birds are heard,<br />\nand animals rest nearby in the shade of Nicula trees on the river banks.<br />\n<br />\nThe gods always play where rivers have for their braclets<br />\nthe sound of curleys and the voice of swans for their speech,<br />\nwater as their garment, carps for their zone,<br />\nthe flowering trees on their banks as earrings,<br />\nthe confluence of rivers as their hips,<br />\nraised sand banks as breasts and plumage of swans their mantle.<br />\n<br />\nThe gods always play where groves are near, rivers, mountains and springs, and in towns with pleasure gardens.\n</p>\n</div><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Brhat Samhita 1.60.4-8, 6th Century AD<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>While major Hindu temples are recommended at sangams (confluence of rivers), river banks, lakes and seashore, <i>Brhat Samhita</i> and <i>Puranas</i> suggest temples may also be built where a natural source of water is not present. Here too, they recommend that a pond be built preferably in front or to the left of the temple with water gardens. If water is neither present naturally nor by design, water is symbolically present at the consecration of temple or the deity. Temples may also be built, suggests <i>Visnudharmottara</i> in Part III of Chapter 93,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> inside caves and carved stones, on hill tops affording peaceful views, mountain slopes overlooking beautiful valleys, inside forests and hermitages, next to gardens, or at the head of a town street.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuals">Manuals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Manuals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Ancient builders of Hindu temples created manuals of architecture, called <i><a href="/wiki/Vastu_shastra" title="Vastu shastra">Vastu-Sastra</a></i> (literally "science" of dwelling; <i>vas-tu</i> is a composite Sanskrit word; <i>vas</i> means "reside", <i>tu</i> means "you"); these contain Vastu-Vidya (literally, knowledge of dwelling).<sup id="cite_ref-bbdutt_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbdutt-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> There exist many Vastu-Sastras on the art of building temples, such as one by <a href="/wiki/Thakkura_Pheru" class="mw-redirect" title="Thakkura Pheru">Thakkura Pheru</a>, describing where and how temples should be built.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakramrisch76_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakramrisch76-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> By the 6th century AD, Sanskrit manuals for in India.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Vastu-Sastra manuals included chapters on home construction, town planning,<sup id="cite_ref-bbdutt_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbdutt-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and how efficient villages, towns and kingdoms integrated temples, water bodies and gardens within them to achieve harmony with nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherri_Silverman_2007_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherri_Silverman_2007-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> While it is unclear, states Barnett,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> as to whether these temple and town planning texts were theoretical studies and if or when they were properly implemented in practice, the manuals suggest that town planning and Hindu temples were conceived as ideals of art and integral part of Hindu social and spiritual life.<sup id="cite_ref-bbdutt_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbdutt-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg/250px-Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg/375px-Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg/500px-Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Kandariya_Mahadeva_temple.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient India produced many Sanskrit manuals for Hindu temple design and construction, covering arrangement of spaces (above) to every aspect of its completion. Yet, the Silpins were given wide latitude to experiment and express their creativity.<sup id="cite_ref-mmgeometry_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmgeometry-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The <i>Silpa Prakasa</i> of Odisha, authored by Ramacandra Bhattaraka Kaulacara in the ninth or tenth centuries AD, is another Sanskrit treatise on Temple Architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-aliceboner66_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aliceboner66-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Silpa Prakasa describes the geometric principles in every aspect of the temple and symbolism such as 16 emotions of human beings carved as 16 types of female figures. These styles were perfected in Hindu temples prevalent in eastern states of India. Other ancient texts found expand these architectural principles, suggesting that different parts of India developed, invented and added their own interpretations. For example, in <i>Saurastra</i> tradition of temple building found in western states of India, the feminine form, expressions and emotions are depicted in 32 types of <i>Nataka-stri</i> compared to 16 types described in <i>Silpa Prakasa</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-aliceboner66_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aliceboner66-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Silpa Prakasa provides brief introduction to 12 types of Hindu temples. Other texts, such as <i>Pancaratra Prasada Prasadhana</i> compiled by Daniel Smith<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and Silpa Ratnakara compiled by Narmada Sankara<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> provide a more extensive list of Hindu temple types.\n</p><p>Ancient Sanskrit manuals for temple construction discovered in Rajasthan, in northwestern region of India, include Sutradhara Mandana\xe2\x80\x99s <i>Prasadamandana</i> (literally, manual for planning and building a temple).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <i>Manasara</i>, a text of South Indian origin, estimated to be in circulation by the 7th century AD, is a guidebook on South Indian temple design and construction.<sup id="cite_ref-susanlchap4_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susanlchap4-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <i>Isanasivagurudeva paddhati</i> is another Sanskrit text from the 9th century describing the art of temple building in India in south and central India.<sup id="cite_ref-skramrisch1958_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skramrisch1958-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In north India, <i>Brihat-samhita</i> by <a href="/wiki/Var%C4%81hamihira" title="Var\xc4\x81hamihira">Var\xc4\x81hamihira</a> is the widely cited ancient Sanskrit manual from 6th century describing the design and construction of <i>Nagara</i> style of Hindu temples.<sup id="cite_ref-mmgeometry_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmgeometry-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hgood_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hgood-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_plan">The plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The plan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">Plan</div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:214px;max-width:214px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:171px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan-temple-konarak.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plan-temple-konarak.png/212px-Plan-temple-konarak.png" width="212" height="172" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plan-temple-konarak.png/318px-Plan-temple-konarak.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Plan-temple-konarak.png/424px-Plan-temple-konarak.png 2x" data-file-width="912" data-file-height="738" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Elements of a Hindu temple in Kalinga style. There are many Hindu temple styles, but they almost universally share common geometric principles, symbolism of ideas, and expression of core beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:174px;max-width:174px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:171px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg/172px-64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg.png" width="172" height="172" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg/258px-64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg/344px-64_grid_Manduka_design_Hindu_Temple_Floor_Plan_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_Ancient_Architecture.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="880" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">The 8x8 (64) grid Manduka Hindu Temple Floor Plan, according to Vastupurusamandala. The 64 grid is the most sacred and common Hindu temple template. The bright saffron center, where diagonals intersect above, represents the Purusha of Hindu philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mmgeometry_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmgeometry-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>A Hindu temple design follows a geometrical design called <i>vastu-purusha-mandala</i>. The name is a composite Sanskrit word with three of the most important components of the plan. <i>Mandala</i> means circle, <i>Purusha</i> is universal essence at the core of Hindu tradition, while <i>Vastu</i> means the dwelling structure.<sup id="cite_ref-sl6869_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sl6869-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Vastupurushamandala is a <a href="/wiki/Yantra" title="Yantra">yantra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakramrisch76_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakramrisch76-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The design lays out a Hindu temple in a symmetrical, self-repeating structure derived from central beliefs, myths, cardinality and mathematical principles.\n</p><p>The four cardinal directions help create the axis of a Hindu temple, around which is formed a perfect square in the space available. The circle of mandala circumscribes the square. The square is considered divine for its perfection and as a symbolic product of knowledge and human thought, while circle is considered earthly, human and observed in everyday life (moon, sun, horizon, water drop, rainbow). Each supports the other.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The square is divided into perfect 64 (or in some cases 81) sub-squares called padas.<sup id="cite_ref-mmgeometry_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmgeometry-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Each pada is conceptually assigned to a symbolic element, sometimes in the form of a deity. The central square(s) of the 64 or 81 grid is dedicated to the <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> (not to be confused with Brahmin), and are called Brahma padas.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a> Sun temples</div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_(6133772365).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_%286133772365%29.jpg/200px-Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_%286133772365%29.jpg" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_%286133772365%29.jpg/300px-Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_%286133772365%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_%286133772365%29.jpg/400px-Martand_Sun_Temple_Central_shrine_%286133772365%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Martand_Sun_Temple" title="Martand Sun Temple">Martand Sun Temple</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg/200px-Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg/300px-Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg/400px-Konark_sun_temple_06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Konark_Sun_Temple" title="Konark Sun Temple">Konark Sun Temple</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Sun_Temple,_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Sun_Temple%2C_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg/200px-Sun_Temple%2C_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg" width="200" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Sun_Temple%2C_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg/300px-Sun_Temple%2C_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Sun_Temple%2C_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg/400px-Sun_Temple%2C_Modhera_-_Sabha_Mandap_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4373" data-file-height="3097" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Sun_Temple,_Modhera" title="Sun Temple, Modhera">Modhera Sun Temple</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Sun_Temple,_Katarmal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sun_Temple%2C_Katarmal.jpg/200px-Sun_Temple%2C_Katarmal.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sun_Temple%2C_Katarmal.jpg/300px-Sun_Temple%2C_Katarmal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Sun_Temple%2C_Katarmal.jpg/400px-Sun_Temple%2C_Katarmal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Katarmal" title="Katarmal">Katarmal Sun Temple</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The 49 grid design is called Sthandila and of great importance in creative expressions of Hindu temples in South India, particularly in \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x98Prakaras\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  The symmetric Vastu-purusa-mandala grids are sometimes combined to form a temple superstructure with two or more attached squares.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The temples face sunrise, and the entrance for the devotee is typically this east side. The mandala pada facing sunrise is dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a> deity (Sun). The Surya pada is flanked by the padas of Satya (Truth) deity on one side and <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a> (king of gods) deity on other. The east and north faces of most temples feature a mix of gods and demi-gods; while west and south feature demons and demi-gods related to the underworld.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> This vastu purusha mandala plan and symbolism is systematically seen in ancient Hindu temples on Indian subcontinent as well as those in southeast Asia, with regional creativity and variations.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Beneath the mandala\xe2\x80\x99s central square(s) is the space for the formless shapeless all pervasive all connecting Universal Spirit, the <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">highest reality</a>, the purusha.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> This space is sometimes referred to as garbha-griya (literally womb house) \xe2\x80\x93 a small, perfect square, windowless, enclosed space without ornamentation that represents universal essence.<sup id="cite_ref-sl6869_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sl6869-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In or near this space is typically a murti (idol). This is the main deity idol, and this varies with each temple. Often it is this idol that gives the temple a local name, such as <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Visnu</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Narayana" title="Narayana">Narayana</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Siva</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Hanuman" title="Hanuman">Hanuman</a> temple, Surya temple, and others.<sup id="cite_ref-gmichell88_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gmichell88-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It is this <a href="/wiki/Garbhagriha" title="Garbhagriha">garbha-griya</a> which devotees seek for \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x98<a href="/wiki/Darshana" class="mw-redirect" title="Darshana">darsana</a>\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 (literally, a sight of knowledge,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> or vision<sup id="cite_ref-sl6869_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sl6869-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>).\n</p><p>Above the vastu-purusha-mandala is a superstructure with a dome called <i><a href="/wiki/Shikhara" title="Shikhara">Shikhara</a></i> in north India, and <i><a href="/wiki/Vimana_(tower)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vimana (tower)">Vimana</a></i> in south India, that stretches towards the sky.<sup id="cite_ref-sl6869_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sl6869-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes, in makeshift temples, the dome may be replaced with symbolic bamboo with few leaves at the top. The vertical dimension\'s cupola or dome is designed as a pyramid, conical or other mountain-like shape, once again using principle of concentric circles and squares (see below).<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Scholars suggest that this shape is inspired by cosmic mountain of Meru or Himalayan Kailasa, the abode of gods according to Vedic mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-sl6869_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sl6869-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg/150px-Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg.png" width="150" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg/225px-Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg/300px-Circle_and_squares_in_Hindu_temple_Spires_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="640" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg/150px-Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg.png" width="150" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg/225px-Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg/300px-Hindu_temple_Spire_design_principle_concentric_circles_squares_Vastu_Purusa_Mandala_without_label.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="640" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">A Hindu temple has a Shikhara (Vimana or Spire) that rises symmetrically above the central core of the temple. These spires come in many designs and shapes, but they all have mathematical precision and geometric symbolism. One of the common principles found in Hindu temple spires is circles and turning-squares theme (left), and a concentric layering design (right) that flows from one to the other as it rises towards the sky.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mwmmountain_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwmmountain-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In larger temples, the central space typically is surrounded by an ambulatory for the devotee to walk around and ritually circumambulate the Purusa, the universal essence.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Often this space is visually decorated with carvings, paintings or images meant to inspire the devotee. In some temples, these images may be stories from Hindu Epics, in others they may be Vedic tales about right and wrong or virtues and vice, in some they may be idols of minor or regional deities. The pillars, walls and ceilings typically also have highly ornate carvings or images of the four just and necessary pursuits of life \xe2\x80\x93 kama, artha, dharma and moksa. This walk around is called <i>pradakshina</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-sl6869_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sl6869-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large temples also have pillared halls called mandapa. One on the east side, serves as the waiting room for pilgrims and devotees. The mandapa may be a separate structure in older temples, but in newer temples this space is integrated into the temple superstructure. Mega temple sites have a main temple surrounded by smaller temples and shrines, but these are still arranged by principles of symmetry, grids and mathematical precision. An important principle found in the layout of Hindu temples is mirroring and repeating fractal-like design structure,<sup id="cite_ref-Trivedi,_K._1989_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trivedi,_K._1989-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> each unique yet also repeating the central common principle, one which Susan Lewandowski refers to as "an organism of repeating cells".<sup id="cite_ref-Susan_Lewandowski_pp_71-73_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Susan_Lewandowski_pp_71-73-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:382px;max-width:382px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg/200px-1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg/300px-1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg/400px-1_Virupaksha_temple_Gopuram_Hampi_Vijayanagar_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:176px;max-width:176px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu_Temple,_Pushkar_(7438731182).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Hindu_Temple%2C_Pushkar_%287438731182%29.jpg/174px-Hindu_Temple%2C_Pushkar_%287438731182%29.jpg" width="174" height="149" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Hindu_Temple%2C_Pushkar_%287438731182%29.jpg/261px-Hindu_Temple%2C_Pushkar_%287438731182%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Hindu_Temple%2C_Pushkar_%287438731182%29.jpg/348px-Hindu_Temple%2C_Pushkar_%287438731182%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2706" data-file-height="2322" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">An illustration of Hindu temple Spires (Shikhara, Vimana) built using concentric circle and rotating-squares principle. The left is from Vijayanagar in <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, the right is from Pushkar in <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The ancient texts on Hindu temple design, the <i>Vastupurusamandala</i> and <i>Vastu Sastras</i>, do not limit themselves to the design of a Hindu temple.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> They describe the temple as a holistic part of its community, and lay out various principles and a diversity of alternate designs for home, village and city layout along with the temple, gardens, water bodies and nature.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1_4-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sherri_Silverman_2007_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherri_Silverman_2007-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Exceptions to the square grid principle</dt></dl>\n<p>Predominant number of Hindu temples exhibit the perfect square grid principle.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> However, there are some exceptions. For example, the Teli-ka-mandir in <a href="/wiki/Gwalior" title="Gwalior">Gwalior</a>, built in the 8th century AD is not a square but is a rectangle in 2:3 proportion. Further, the temple explores a number of structures and shrines in 1:1, 1:2, 1:3, 2:5, 3:5 and 4:5 ratios. These ratios are exact, suggesting the architect intended to use these harmonic ratios, and the rectangle pattern was not a mistake, nor an arbitrary approximation. Other examples of non-square harmonic ratios are found at Naresar temple site of Madhya Pradesh and Nakti-Mata temple near Jaipur, Rajasthan. <a href="/wiki/Michael_W._Meister" title="Michael W. Meister">Michael Meister</a> suggests that these exceptions mean the ancient Sanskrit manuals for temple building were guidelines, and Hinduism permitted its artisans flexibility in expression and aesthetic independence.<sup id="cite_ref-mmgeometry_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mmgeometry-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_symbolism">The symbolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The symbolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A Hindu temple is a symbolic reconstruction of the universe and universal principles that make everything in it function.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakvol1011_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakvol1011-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The temples reflect Hindu philosophy and its diverse views on cosmos and Truths.<sup id="cite_ref-Trivedi,_K._1989_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trivedi,_K._1989-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hinduism has no traditional ecclesiastical order, no centralized religious authorities, no governing body, no prophet(s) nor any binding holy book; Hindus can choose to be polytheistic, pantheistic, monistic, or atheistic.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Within this diffuse and open structure, spirituality in Hindu philosophy is an individual experience, and referred to as k\xe1\xb9\xa3aitraj\xc3\xb1a (Sanskrit: \xe0\xa4\x95\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb7\xe0\xa5\x88\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\x9c\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\x9e<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>). It defines spiritual practice as one\xe2\x80\x99s journey towards <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>, awareness of self, the discovery of higher truths, true nature of reality, and a consciousness that is liberated and content.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gf_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gf-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> A Hindu temple reflects these core beliefs. The central core of almost all Hindu temples is not a large communal space; the temple is designed for the individual, a couple or a family \xe2\x80\x93 a small, private space where he or she experiences darsana.\n</p><p>Darsana is itself a symbolic word. In ancient Hindu scripts, darsana is the name of six methods or alternate viewpoints of understanding Truth.<sup id="cite_ref-skv189_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skv189-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> These are Nyaya, Vaisesika, Sankhya, Yoga, Mimamsa and Vedanta \xe2\x80\x93 each of which flowered into their own schools of Hinduism, each of which are considered valid, alternate paths to understanding Truth and realizing Self in the Hindu way of life.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple,_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple%2C_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg/220px-%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple%2C_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple%2C_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg/330px-%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple%2C_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple%2C_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg/440px-%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple%2C_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1212" data-file-height="1812" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%271%27_Sun_Temple_Konark_Temple,_Kama_Love_Orissa_India_February_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>K\xc4\x81ma is celebrated in some Hindu temples, such as <a href="/wiki/Khajuraho_Group_of_Monuments" title="Khajuraho Group of Monuments">Khajuraho</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Konark" title="Konark">Konark</a> Temple (above).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>From names to forms, from images to stories carved into the walls of a temple, symbolism is everywhere in a Hindu temple. Life principles such as the pursuit of joy, sex, connection and emotional pleasure (kama) are fused into mystical, erotic and architectural forms in Hindu temples. These motifs and principles of human life are part of the sacred texts of Hindu, such as its Upanishads; the temples express these same principles in a different form, through art and spaces. For example, Brihadaranyaka Upanisad at 4.3.21, recites:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>In the embrace of his beloved a man forgets the whole world,<br />\neverything both within and without;<br />\nin the same way, he who embraces the Self,<br />\nknows neither within nor without.\n</p>\n</div><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Brihadaranyaka_Upanishad" title="Brihadaranyaka Upanishad">Brihadaranyaka Upanishad</a>, 7th Century BC<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The architecture of Hindu temples is also symbolic. The whole structure fuses the daily life and it surroundings with the divine concepts, through a structure that is open yet raised on a terrace, transitioning from the secular towards the sacred,<sup id="cite_ref-eleach1983_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleach1983-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> inviting the visitor inwards towards the Brahma pada and temple\xe2\x80\x99s central core, as well as lifting him upwards into a symbolic space marked by its spire (shikhara, vimana). The ancient temples had grand intricately carved entrances but no doors, and lacked a boundary wall. In most cultures, suggests <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Leach" title="Edmund Leach">Edmund Leach</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-eleach1983_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleach1983-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> a boundary and gateway separates the secular and the sacred, and this gateway door is grand. In Hindu tradition, this is discarded in favor of an open and diffusive architecture, where the secular world was not separated from the sacred, but transitioned and flowed into the sacred.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The Hindu temple has structural walls, which were patterned usually within the 64 grid, or other geometric layouts. Yet the layout was open on all sides, except for the core space which had just one opening for darsana.  The temple space is laid out in a series of courts (mandappas). The outermost regions may incorporate the negative and suffering side of life with symbolism of evil, asuras and rakshashas (demons); but in small temples this layer is dispensed with. When present, this outer region diffuse into the next inner layer that bridges as human space, followed by another inner Devika padas space and symbolic arts incorporating the positive and joyful side of life about the good and the gods. This divine space then concentrically diffuses inwards and lifts the guest to the core of the temple, where resides the main idol as well as the space for the Purusa and ideas held to be most sacred principles in Hindu tradition. The symbolism in the arts and temples of Hinduism, suggests Edmund Leach, is similar to those in Christianity and other major religions of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_teams_that_built_Hindu_temples">The teams that built Hindu temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The teams that built Hindu temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Inside Hindu temples</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse;text-align:center;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;margin:auto;width:320px"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Murufgan_Temple_inside_the_Big_Temple.JPG" class="image"><img //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Relief_Prambanan.jpg/320px-Relief_Prambanan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px"><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a> scene along padas</div></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Rameswaram Temple Inside.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg/160px-Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg" width="160" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg/240px-Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg/320px-Rameswaram_Temple_Inside.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">Outer hall, <a href="/wiki/Rameswaram_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Rameswaram Temple">Rameswaram</a></div></td><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A sanctum inside the Hoysaleshwara temple in Halebidu.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg/160px-A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg" width="160" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg/240px-A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg/320px-A_sanctum_inside_the_Hoysaleshwara_temple_in_Halebidu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1916" data-file-height="1438" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">Center, <a href="/wiki/Hoysaleswara_Temple" title="Hoysaleswara Temple">Hoysaleswara</a></div></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Indian texts call the craftsmen and builders of temples as \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x98Silpin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 (Sanskrit: \xe0\xa4\xb6\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xb2\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa5\x8d<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>), derived from \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x98Silpa\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99.<sup id="cite_ref-sparker1987_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sparker1987-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> One of earliest mentions of Sanskrit word Silpa is in <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>, from about 1000 BC, which scholars have translated as any work of art.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars suggest that the word Silpa has no direct one word translation in English, nor does the word \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x98Silpin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99. Silpa, explains Stella Kramrisch,<sup id="cite_ref-skramrisch1958_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skramrisch1958-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> is a multicolored word and incorporates art, skill, craft, ingenuity, imagination, form, expression and inventiveness of any art or craft. Similarly a Shilpin, notes Kramrisch, is a complex Sanskrit word, describing any person who embodies art, science, culture, skill, rhythm and employs creative principles to produce any divine form of expression. Silpins who built Hindu temples, as well as the art works and sculpture within them, were considered by the ancient Sanskrit texts to deploy arts whose number are unlimited, Kala (techniques) that were 64 in number,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and Vidya (science) that were of 32 types.<sup id="cite_ref-skramrisch1958_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skramrisch1958-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Rani_ki_vav1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Rani_ki_vav1.jpg/200px-Rani_ki_vav1.jpg" width="200" height="157" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Rani_ki_vav1.jpg/300px-Rani_ki_vav1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Rani_ki_vav1.jpg/400px-Rani_ki_vav1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="9473" data-file-height="7427" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Rani_ki_vav" title="Rani ki vav">Rani ki vav stepwell</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG/200px-View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG" width="200" height="98" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG/300px-View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG/400px-View_of_Lord_Nataraja_at_Temple_museum.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4804" data-file-height="2354" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Meenakshi_Amman_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Meenakshi Amman Temple">Hall of Thousand Pillars at Meenakshi Amman Temple</a></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The Hindu manuals of temple construction describe the education, characteristics of good artists and architects. The general education of a Hindu Shilpin in ancient India included Lekha or Lipi (alphabet, reading and writing), Rupa (drawing and geometry), Ganana (arithmetic). These were imparted from age 5 to 12. The advanced students would continue in higher stages of Shilpa Sastra studies till the age of 25.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Apart from specialist technical competence, the manuals suggest that best Silpins for building a Hindu temple are those who know the essence of Vedas and Agamas, consider themselves as students, keep well verse with principles of traditional sciences and mathematics, painting and geography.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakramrisch76_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakramrisch76-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Further they are kind, free from jealousy, righteous, have their sense under control, of happy disposition, and ardent in everything they do.<sup id="cite_ref-skramrisch1958_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skramrisch1958-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Silparatna, a Hindu temple project would start with a Yajamana (patron), and include a Sthapaka (guru, spiritual guide and architect-priest), a Sthapati (architect) who would design the building, a Sutragrahin (surveyor), and many Vardhakins (workers, masons, painters, plasterers, overseers) and Taksakas (sculptors).<sup id="cite_ref-stellakramrisch76_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakramrisch76-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hgood_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hgood-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> While the temple is under construction, all those working on the temple were revered and considered sacerdotal by the patron as well as others witnessing the construction.<sup id="cite_ref-sparker1987_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sparker1987-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Further, it was a tradition that all tools and materials used in temple building and all creative work had the sanction of a sacrament.<sup id="cite_ref-stellakramrisch76_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stellakramrisch76-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> For example, if a carpenter or sculptor needed to fell a tree or cut a rock from a hill, he would propitiate the tree or rock with prayers, seeking forgiveness for cutting it from its surroundings, and explaining his intent and purpose. The axe used to cut the tree would be anointed with butter to minimize the hurt to the tree.<sup id="cite_ref-skramrisch1958_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skramrisch1958-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Even in modern times, in some parts of India such as <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, Visvakarma Puja is a ritual festival every year where the craftsmen and artists worship their arts, tools and materials.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_functions_of_Hindu_temples">Social functions of Hindu temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Social functions of Hindu temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_Gopurams" title="List of tallest Gopurams">Tall Hindu structures</a></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:129px;max-width:129px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:192px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Murudeshwara_raja_gopura_HDR,_Jul_2012.jpg"
