height="192" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Murudeshwara_raja_gopura_HDR%2C_Jul_2012.jpg/191px-Murudeshwara_raja_gopura_HDR%2C_Jul_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Murudeshwara_raja_gopura_HDR%2C_Jul_2012.jpg/254px-Murudeshwara_raja_gopura_HDR%2C_Jul_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2926" data-file-height="4428" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">Raja Gopura is a 20-storied <a href="/wiki/Gopura" class="mw-redirect" title="Gopura">gopura</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Murdeshwar" title="Murdeshwar">Murdeshwar</a> temple.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:259px;max-width:259px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:192px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Chaturbhuj_Temple,_Orchha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Chaturbhuj_Temple%2C_Orchha.jpg/257px-Chaturbhuj_Temple%2C_Orchha.jpg" width="257" height="193" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Chaturbhuj_Temple%2C_Orchha.jpg/386px-Chaturbhuj_Temple%2C_Orchha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Chaturbhuj_Temple%2C_Orchha.jpg/514px-Chaturbhuj_Temple%2C_Orchha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Chaturbhuj_Temple_(Orchha)" title="Chaturbhuj Temple (Orchha)">Chaturbhuj Temple</a> built by the <a href="/wiki/Bundela" title="Bundela">Bundela</a> Rajputs, is one of the tallest pre-modern structure in the Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsher200357_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsher200357-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:193px;max-width:193px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:127px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg/191px-New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg" width="191" height="128" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg/287px-New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg/382px-New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6016" data-file-height="4016" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Shri_Vishwanath_Mandir" title="Shri Vishwanath Mandir">Shri Vishwanath Mandir</a> is situated in <a href="/wiki/Benaras_Hindu_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Benaras Hindu University">Benaras Hindu University</a> and has the tallest temple tower in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_description_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief_description-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:195px;max-width:195px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:127px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple,_dedicated_to_Vishnu,_in_Srirangam,_near_Tiruchirappali_(24)_(37254366620).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple%2C_dedicated_to_Vishnu%2C_in_Srirangam%2C_near_Tiruchirappali_%2824%29_%2837254366620%29.jpg/193px-Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple%2C_dedicated_to_Vishnu%2C_in_Srirangam%2C_near_Tiruchirappali_%2824%29_%2837254366620%29.jpg" width="193" height="127" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple%2C_dedicated_to_Vishnu%2C_in_Srirangam%2C_near_Tiruchirappali_%2824%29_%2837254366620%29.jpg/290px-Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple%2C_dedicated_to_Vishnu%2C_in_Srirangam%2C_near_Tiruchirappali_%2824%29_%2837254366620%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple%2C_dedicated_to_Vishnu%2C_in_Srirangam%2C_near_Tiruchirappali_%2824%29_%2837254366620%29.jpg/386px-Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple%2C_dedicated_to_Vishnu%2C_in_Srirangam%2C_near_Tiruchirappali_%2824%29_%2837254366620%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5940" data-file-height="3910" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Ranganathaswamy_Temple,_Srirangam" title="Ranganathaswamy Temple, Srirangam">Ranganathaswamy Temple</a> is one of the largest Hindu temple in the world and it houses the tallest <a href="/wiki/Gopuram" title="Gopuram">gopuram</a> in India.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Hindu temples served as nuclei of important social, economic, artistic and intellectual functions in ancient and medieval India.<sup id="cite_ref-michelltempleschool_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michelltempleschool-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Burton_Stein" title="Burton Stein">Burton Stein</a> states that South Indian temples managed regional development function, such as irrigation projects, land reclamation, post-disaster relief and recovery. These activities were paid for by the donations (melvarum) they collected from devotees.<sup id="cite_ref-bstein_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bstein-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> According to James Heitzman, these donations came from a wide spectrum of the Indian society, ranging from kings, queens, officials in the kingdom to merchants, priests and shepherds.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Temples also managed lands endowed to it by its devotees upon their death. They would provide employment to the poorest.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Some temples had large treasury, with gold and silver coins, and these temples served as banks.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hindu temples over time became wealthy from grants and donations from royal patrons as well as private individuals. Major temples became employers and patrons of economic activity. They sponsored land reclamation and infrastructure improvements, states Michell, including building facilities such as water tanks, irrigation canals and new roads.<sup id="cite_ref-michell58_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michell58-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> A very detailed early record from 1101 lists over 600 employees (excluding the priests) of the <a href="/wiki/Brihadisvara_Temple,_Thanjavur" title="Brihadisvara Temple, Thanjavur">Brihadisvara Temple, Thanjavur</a>, still one of the largest temples in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>. Most worked part-time and received the use of temple farmland as reward.<sup id="cite_ref-michell58_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michell58-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> For those thus employed by the temple, according to Michell, "some gratuitous services were usually considered obligatory, such as dragging the temple chariots on festival occasions and helping when a large building project was undertaken".<sup id="cite_ref-michell58_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michell58-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Temples also acted as refuge during times of political unrest and danger.<sup id="cite_ref-michell58_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michell58-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In contemporary times, the process of building a Hindu temple by emigrants and diasporas from South Asia has also served as a process of building a community, a social venue to network, reduce prejudice and seek civil rights together.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Library_of_manuscripts">Library of manuscripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Library of manuscripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Types of Hindu temples</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse;text-align:center;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;margin:auto;width:320px"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Undavalli_Caves.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Undavalli Caves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Undavalli_Caves.jpg/240px-Undavalli_Caves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Undavalli_Caves.jpg/320px-Undavalli_Caves.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3061" data-file-height="2038" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">Cave temple</div></td><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Hindu temple in Ubud.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg/160px-Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg" width="160" height="97" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg/240px-Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg/320px-Hindu_temple_in_Ubud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1589" data-file-height="963" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">Forest temple</div></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Masrur rockcut temple.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg/160px-Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg" width="160" height="107" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg/240px-Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg/320px-Masrur_rockcut_temple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6144" data-file-height="4096" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">Mountain temple</div></td><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Somnath-current.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Somnath-current.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Somnath-current.jpg/160px-Somnath-current.jpg" width="160" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Somnath-current.jpg/240px-Somnath-current.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Somnath-current.jpg/320px-Somnath-current.jpg 2x" data-file-width="712" data-file-height="534" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">Seashore temple</div></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>John Guy and Jorrit Britschgi state Hindu temples served as centers where ancient manuscripts were routinely used for learning and where the texts were copied when they wore out.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In South India, temples and associated <i>mutts</i> served custodial functions, and a large number of manuscripts on <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>, poetry, grammar and other subjects were written, multiplied and preserved inside the temples.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Archaeological and epigraphical evidence indicates existence of libraries called <i>Sarasvati-bhandara</i>, dated possibly to early 12th-century and employing librarians, attached to Hindu temples.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Palm-leaf manuscripts called <a href="/wiki/Palm-leaf_manuscript#Javanese_and_Balinese" title="Palm-leaf manuscript">lontar</a> in dedicated stone libraries have been discovered by archaeologists at Hindu temples in <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a> Indonesia and in 10th century Cambodian temples such as Angkor Wat and <a href="/wiki/Banteay_Srei" title="Banteay Srei">Banteay Srei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temple_schools">Temple schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Temple schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Inscriptions from the 4th century AD suggest the existence of schools around Hindu temples, called <i>Ghatikas</i> or <i>Mathas</i>, where the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> were studied.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In south India, 9th century Vedic schools attached to Hindu temples were called <i>Calai</i> or <i>Salai</i>, and these provided free boarding and lodging to students and scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dayalan1992p202_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dayalan1992p202-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The temples linked to <a href="/wiki/Bhakti_movement" title="Bhakti movement">Bhakti movement</a> in the early 2nd millennium, were dominated by non-Brahmins.<sup id="cite_ref-scharfetempleschools_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scharfetempleschools-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> These assumed many educational functions, including the exposition, recitation and public discourses of Sanskrit and Vedic texts.<sup id="cite_ref-scharfetempleschools_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scharfetempleschools-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Some temple schools offered wide range of studies, ranging from Hindu scriptures to Buddhist texts, grammar, philosophy, martial arts, music and painting.<sup id="cite_ref-michelltempleschool_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michelltempleschool-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scharfetempleschools2_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scharfetempleschools2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> By the 8th century, Hindu temples also served as the social venue for tests, debates, team competition and Vedic recitals called <i>Anyonyam</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-michelltempleschool_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michelltempleschool-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-scharfetempleschools2_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scharfetempleschools2-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Hospitals.2C_community_kitchen.2C_monasteries"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hospitals,_community_kitchen,_monasteries">Hospitals, community kitchen, monasteries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hospitals, community kitchen, monasteries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>According to Kenneth G. Zysk \xe2\x80\x93 a professor specializing in Indology and ancient medicine, Hindu <i>mathas</i> and temples had by the 10th-century attached medical care along with their religious and educational roles.<sup id="cite_ref-Zysk1998p45_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zysk1998p45-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This is evidenced by various inscriptions found in Bengal, Andhra Pradesh and elsewhere. An inscription dated to about AD 930 states the provision of a physician to two <i>matha</i> to care for the sick and destitute. Another inscription dated to 1069 at a Vishnu temple in Tamil Nadu describes a hospital attached to the temple, listing the nurses, physicians, medicines and beds for patients. Similarly, a stone inscription in Andhra Pradesh dated to about 1262 mentions the provision of a <i>prasutishala</i> (maternity house), <i>vaidya</i> (physician), an <i>arogyashala</i> (health house) and a <i>viprasattra</i> (hospice, kitchen) with the religious center where people from all social backgrounds could be fed and cared for.<sup id="cite_ref-Zysk1998p45_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zysk1998p45-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> According to Zysk, both Buddhist monasteries and Hindu religious centers provided facilities to care for the sick and needy in the 1st millennium, but with the destruction of Buddhist centers after the 12th century, the Hindu religious institutions assumed these social responsibilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Zysk1998p45_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zysk1998p45-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> According to George Michell, Hindu temples in South India were active charity centers and they provided free meal for wayfarers, pilgrims and devotees, as well as boarding facilities for students and hospitals for the sick.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 15th and 16th century Hindu temples at <a href="/wiki/Hampi" title="Hampi">Hampi</a> featured storage spaces (temple granary, <i>kottara</i>), water tanks and kitchens.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Many major pilgrimage sites have featured <i>dharmashalas</i> since early times. These were attached to Hindu temples, particularly in South India, providing a bed and meal to pilgrims. They relied on any voluntary donation the visitor may leave and to land grants from local rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-sen126_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sen126-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Some temples have operated their kitchens on daily basis to serve the visitor and the needy, while others during major community gatherings or festivals. Examples include the major kitchens run by Hindu temples in <a href="/wiki/Udupi" title="Udupi">Udupi</a> (Karnataka), <a href="/wiki/Puri" title="Puri">Puri</a> (Odisha) and <a href="/wiki/Tirupati" title="Tirupati">Tirupati</a> (Andhra Pradesh). The tradition of sharing food in smaller temple is typically called <i>prasada</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-sen126_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sen126-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles">Styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Golden Hindu Temples</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse;text-align:center;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;margin:auto;width:360px"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:180px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:180px;height:180px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Sripuram_Temple_Full_View.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Sripuram Temple Full View.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Sripuram_Temple_Full_View.jpg/180px-Sripuram_Temple_Full_View.jpg" width="180" height="121" style="padding:1px"><a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple,_Sripuram" title="Golden Temple, Sripuram">The Golden Temple</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vellore" title="Vellore">Vellore</a> is gilded with 1500 kg of pure gold.</div></td><td style="width:180px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:180px;height:180px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Benares-_The_Golden_Temple%2C_India%2C_ca._1915_%28IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66%29.jpg/226px-Benares-_The_Golden_Temple%2C_India%2C_ca._1915_%28IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Benares-_The_Golden_Temple%2C_India%2C_ca._1915_%28IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66%29.jpg/301px-Benares-_The_Golden_Temple%2C_India%2C_ca._1915_%28IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="3829" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px">One tonne of gold for plating of <a href="/wiki/Kashi_Vishwanath_Temple" title="Kashi Vishwanath Temple">Kashi Vishwanath Temple</a>\'s golden dome, was donated by Maharaja <a href="/wiki/Ranjit_Singh" title="Ranjit Singh">Ranjit Singh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Madhuri_2007_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madhuri_2007-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></div></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Hindu temples are found in diverse locations each incorporating different methods of construction and styles:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Mountain<sup id="cite_ref-mwmmountain_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwmmountain-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> temples such as Masrur</li>\n<li>Cave<sup id="cite_ref-mwmcaveforest_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwmcaveforest-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> temples such as Chandrabhaga, Chalukya<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> and Ellora</li>\n<li>Step well temple compounds such as the Mata Bhavani, Ankol Mata and Huccimallugudi.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Forest<sup id="cite_ref-mwmcaveforest_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwmcaveforest-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> temples such as Kasaun and Kusama<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>River bank and sea shore temples such as Somnath.</li></ul>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg/200px-Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg/300px-Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg/400px-Rani_ki_vav_07.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5104" data-file-height="3403" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg/200px-Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg/300px-Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg/400px-Rani_ki_Vav_sculptures_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2888" data-file-height="1926" /></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Hindu deities, stepwell style.</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>Step well temples</dt></dl>\n<p>In arid western parts of India, such as Rajasthan and Gujarat, Hindu communities built large walk in wells that served as the only source of water in dry months but also served as social meeting places and carried religious significance. These monuments went down into earth towards subterranean water, up to seven storey, and were part of a temple complex.<sup id="cite_ref-jneubauer_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jneubauer-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> These vav (literally, stepwells) had intricate art reliefs on the walls, with numerous idols and images of Hindu deities, water spirits and erotic symbolism. The step wells were named after Hindu deities; for example, <a href="/wiki/Mata_Bhavani%27s_Stepwell" title="Mata Bhavani&#39;s Stepwell">Mata Bhavani\'s Stepwell</a>, Ankol Mata Vav, Sikotari Vav and others.<sup id="cite_ref-jneubauer_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jneubauer-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The temple ranged from being small single pada (cell) structure to large nearby complexes. These stepwells and their temple compounds have been variously dated from late 1st millennium BC through 11th century AD. Of these, <a href="/wiki/Rani_ki_vav" title="Rani ki vav">Rani ki vav</a>, with hundreds of art reliefs including many of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> deity avatars, has been declared a UNESCO World Heritage site.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Cave Temples</dt></dl>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_rock-cut_architecture" title="Indian rock-cut architecture">Indian rock-cut architecture</a> evolved in Maharashtran temple style in the 1st millennium AD. The temples are carved from a single piece of rock as a complete temple or carved in a cave to look like the interior of a temple. <a href="/wiki/Ellora" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellora">Ellora</a> Temple is an example of the former, while The <a href="/wiki/Elephanta_Caves" title="Elephanta Caves">Elephanta Caves</a> are representative of the latter style.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Elephanta_Caves" title="Elephanta Caves">Elephanta Caves</a> consist of two groups of caves\xe2\x80\x94the first is a large group of five Hindu caves and the second is a smaller group of two Buddhist caves. The Hindu caves contain rock-cut stone sculptures, representing the Shaiva Hindu sect, dedicated to the god Shiva.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_inside_Hindu_temples">Arts inside Hindu temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Arts inside Hindu temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail,_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura,_Karnataka,_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail%2C_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg/220px-12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail%2C_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail%2C_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg/330px-12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail%2C_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail%2C_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg/440px-12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail%2C_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura%2C_Karnataka%2C_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3154" data-file-height="4732" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:12th_century_carving_exquisite_detail,_right_down_to_her_fingernails_Chennakesava_temple_at_Somanathapura,_Karnataka,_India.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of Chitrardha style of art work in a Hindu temple.</div></div></div>\n<p>A typical, ancient Hindu temple has a profusion of arts \xe2\x80\x93 from paintings to sculpture, from symbolic icons to engravings, from thoughtful layout of space to fusion of mathematical principles with Hindu sense of time and cardinality.\n</p><p>Ancient Sanskrit texts classify idols and images in number of ways. For example, one method of classification is the dimensionality of completion:<sup id="cite_ref-grao1914_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grao1914-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Chitra</b> \xe2\x80\x93 images that are 3-dimensional and completely formed,</li>\n<li><b>Chitrardha</b> \xe2\x80\x93 images that are engraved in half relief,</li>\n<li><b>Chitrabhasa</b> \xe2\x80\x93 images that are 2-dimensional such as paintings on walls and cloths.</li></ul>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:253px;max-width:253px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/120px-Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg" width="120" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/180px-Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/240px-Kali_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1472" data-file-height="2048" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/125px-Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg" width="125" height="167" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/188px-Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg/250px-Saraswati_by_Raja_Ravi_Varma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3072" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Images and idols inside Hindu temples vary widely in their expression. Raudra or ugra images express destruction, fear and violence, such as Kali image on left. Shanta or saumya images express joy, knowledge and harmony, such as Saraswati image on right. Saumya images are most common in Hindu temples.</div></div></div>\n<p>Another way of classification is by the expressive state of the image:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Raudra</b> or <b>ugra</b> \xe2\x80\x93 are images that were meant to terrify, induce fear. These typically have wide, circular eyes, carry weapons, have skulls and bones as adornment. These idols were worshiped by soldiers before going to war, or by people in times of distress or terrors. Raudra deity temples were not set up inside villages or towns, but invariably outside and in remote areas of a kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-grao1914_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grao1914-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Shanta</b> and <b>saumya</b> \xe2\x80\x93 are images that were pacific, peaceful and expressive of love, compassion, kindness and other virtues in Hindu pantheon. These images would carry symbolic icons of peace, knowledge, music, wealth, flowers, sensuality among other things. In ancient India, these temples were predominant inside villages and towns.<sup id="cite_ref-grao1914_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grao1914-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>A Hindu temple may or may not include an idol or images, but larger temples usually do. Personal Hindu temples at home or a hermitage may have a pada for yoga or meditation, but be devoid of anthropomorphic representations of god. Nature or others arts may surround him or her. To a Hindu yogin, states Gopinath Rao,<sup id="cite_ref-grao1914_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grao1914-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> one who has realised Self and the Universal Principle within himself, there is no need for any temple or divine image for worship. However, for those who have yet to reach this height of realization, various symbolic manifestations through images, idols and icons as well as mental modes of worship are offered as one of the spiritual paths in the Hindu way of life. This belief is repeated in ancient Hindu scriptures. For example, the Jabaladarshana Upanishad states:<sup id="cite_ref-grao1914_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grao1914-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">\n<p>\xe0\xa4\xb6\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xb5\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\xbf \xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xb6\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xaf\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa4\xbf \xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xb8\xe0\xa5\x81 \xe0\xa4\xa8 \xe0\xa4\xaf\xe0\xa5\x8b\xe0\xa4\x97\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\x83 |<br />\n\xe0\xa4\x85\xe0\xa4\x9c\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\x9e\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\x82 \xe0\xa4\xad\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xb5\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xa5\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xaf \xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\x83 \xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\x95\xe0\xa4\xb2\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\x83 || \xe0\xa5\xab\xe0\xa5\xaf ||<br />\n- \xe0\xa4\x9c\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xac\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xb2\xe0\xa4\xa6\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb6\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa5\x8b\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xb7\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa5\x8d\n</p>\n</div>\n<div class="poem">\n<p>A yogin perceives god (Siva) within himself,<br />\nimages are for those who have not reached this knowledge. (Verse 59)\n</p>\n</div><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Jabaladarsana Upanishad, <sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_development_and_destruction">Historical development and destruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Historical development and destruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Ancient Hindu temples outside the Indian subcontinent</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse;text-align:center;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;margin:auto;width:320px"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Angkor_Wat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Angkor Wat.jpg" data-file-width="2004" data-file-height="1362" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px"><a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia"
