style="padding:1px"><a href="/wiki/Prambanan" title="Prambanan">Prambanan</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div></td><td style="width:160px;text-align:center;:"><div style="display:table-cell;vertical-align:middle;width:160px;height:120px;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto"><a href="/wiki/File:Po_Nagar_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Po Nagar 1.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Po_Nagar_1.jpg/160px-Po_Nagar_1.jpg" width="160" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Po_Nagar_1.jpg/240px-Po_Nagar_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Po_Nagar_1.jpg/320px-Po_Nagar_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a></div><div style="padding:1px"><a href="/wiki/Po_Nagar" title="Po Nagar">Po Nagar</a> temple, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a></div></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A number of ancient Indian texts suggest the prevalence of idols, temples and shrines in Indian subcontinent for thousands of years. For example, the 4th century BC text, <i>Astadhyayi</i> mentions male deity <i>arcas</i> (images/idols) of Agni, Indra, Varuna, Rudra, Mrda, Pusa, Surya, Soma being worshipped, as well as the worship of arcas of female goddesses such as Indrani, Varunani, Usa, Bhavani, Prthivi and Vrsakapayi.<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The 2nd Century BC \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x98Mahabhasya\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99 of Patanjali extensively describes temples of <i>Dhanapati</i> (deity of wealth and finance, Kubera), as well as temples of Rama and Kesava, wherein the worship included dance, music and extensive rituals. The <i>Mahabhasya</i> also describes the rituals for Krsna, Visnu and Siva. An image recovered from Mathura in north India has been dated to the 2nd century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Kautilya\xe2\x80\x99s <a href="/wiki/Arthashastra" title="Arthashastra">Arthashastra</a> from 3rd Century BC describes a city of temples, each enshrining various Vedic and Puranic deities. All three of these sources have common names, describe common rituals, symbolism and significance possibly suggesting that the idea of idols, temples and shrines passed from one generation to next, in ancient India, at least from the 4th century BC.<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The oldest temples, suggest scholars, were built of <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">brick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dimension_stone" title="Dimension stone">Stone</a> became the preferred material of construction later.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Hindu_Temple_1946_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Hindu_Temple_1946-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early Jainism and Buddhism literature, along with Kautilya\xe2\x80\x99s Arthashastra, describe structures, embellishments and designs of these temples \xe2\x80\x93 all with motifs and deities currently prevalent in Hinduism. Bas-reliefs and idols have been found from 2nd to 3rd Century, but none of the temple structures have survived. Scholars<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> theorize that those ancient temples of India, later referred to as Hindu temples, were modeled after domestic structure \xe2\x80\x93 a house or a palace. Beyond shrines, nature was revered, in forms such as trees, rivers, stupas before the time of Buddha and Vardhamana Mahavira. As Jainism and Buddhism branched off from the religious tradition later to be called Hinduism, the ideas, designs and plans of ancient Vedic and Upanishad era shrines were adopted and evolved, likely from the competitive development of temples and arts in Jainism and Buddhism. Ancient reliefs found so far, states Michael Meister,<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> suggest five basic shrine designs and combinations thereof in 1st millennium BC:\n</p>\n<ol><li>A raised platform with or without a symbol</li>\n<li>A raised platform under an umbrella</li>\n<li>A raised platform under a tree</li>\n<li>A raised platform enclosed with a railing</li>\n<li>A raised platform inside a pillared pavilion</li></ol>\n<p>Many of these ancient shrines were roofless, some had <i>toranas</i> and roof.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lad_NKAD90.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Lad_NKAD90.JPG/220px-Lad_NKAD90.JPG" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Lad_NKAD90.JPG/330px-Lad_NKAD90.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Lad_NKAD90.JPG/440px-Lad_NKAD90.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1490" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lad_NKAD90.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ladkhan Shiva Temple in Karnataka from the 5th century.</div></div></div>\n<p>From the 1st century BC through 3rd Century AD, the evidence and details about ancient temples increases. The ancient literature refers to these temples as <i>Pasada</i> (or Prasada), <i>stana</i>, <i>mahasthana</i>, <i>devalaya</i>, <i>devagrha</i>, <i>devakula</i>, <i>devakulika</i>, <i>ayatana</i> and <i>harmya</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The entrance of the temple is referred to as <i>dvarakosthaka</i> in these ancient texts notes Meister,<sup id="cite_ref-mm1988e_112-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mm1988e-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> the temple hall is described as <i>sabha</i> or <i>ayagasabha</i>, pillars were called <i>kumbhaka</i>, while <i>vedika</i> referred to the structures at the boundary of a temple.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Durga_Temple_Ceiling,_Aihole,_Karnataka.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Durga_Temple_Ceiling%2C_Aihole%2C_Karnataka.jpg/220px-Durga_Temple_Ceiling%2C_Aihole%2C_Karnataka.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Durga_Temple_Ceiling%2C_Aihole%2C_Karnataka.jpg/330px-Durga_Temple_Ceiling%2C_Aihole%2C_Karnataka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Durga_Temple_Ceiling%2C_Aihole%2C_Karnataka.jpg/440px-Durga_Temple_Ceiling%2C_Aihole%2C_Karnataka.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1023" data-file-height="656" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Durga_Temple_Ceiling,_Aihole,_Karnataka.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 7th century Chalukyan style temple ceiling in Karnataka.</div></div></div>\n<p>With the start of Gupta dynasty in the 4th century, Hindu temples flourished in innovation, design, scope, form, use of stone and new materials as well as symbolic synthesis of culture and <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharmic</a> principles with artistic expression.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> It is this period that is credited with the ideas of garbhagrha for <i>Purusa</i>, mandapa for sheltering the devotees and rituals in progress, as well as symbolic motifs relating to dharma, karma, kama, artha and moksha. Temple superstructures were built from stone, brick and wide range of materials. Entrance ways, walls and pillars were intricately carved, while parts of temple were decorated with gold, silver and jewels. Visnu, Siva and other deities were placed in Hindu temples, while Buddhists and Jains built their own temples, often side by side with Hindus.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 4th through 6th century marked the flowering of <i>Vidharbha</i> style, whose accomplishments survive in central India as Ajanta caves, Pavnar, Mandhal and Mahesvar. In South India, this period is credited with some of the earliest stone temples of the region, with Chalukya temples dated to be from the 5th century by some scholars,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> and the 6th by some others.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Over 6th and 7th century, temple designs were further refined during Maurya dynasty, evidence of which survives today at Ellora and Elephanta.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg/220px-Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg/330px-Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg/440px-Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg 2x" data-file-width="685" data-file-height="712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_Of_The_Ancient_Temple_Of_Vishveshvur_by_James_Prinsep_1832.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many Hindu temples were destroyed and the remains used to rebuild Islamic mosques between 12th and 18th century AD. Above drawing by <a href="/wiki/James_Prinsep" title="James Prinsep">James Prinsep</a> (1832) shows an overlay of a mosque built over the ancient Hindu Vishveshvur temple.</div></div></div>\n<p>It is the 5th through 7th century AD when outer design and appearances of Hindu temples in north India and south India began to widely diverge.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the forms, theme, symbolism and central ideas in the grid design remained same, before and after, pan-India as innovations were adopted to give distinctly different visual expressions.\n</p><p>During the 5th to 11th century, Hindu temples flourished outside Indian subcontinent, such as in Cambodia, Viet Nam, Malaysia and Indonesia.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Destruction and conversion</dt></dl>\n<p>With the arrival of Islam in South Asia, Hindu temples along with the temples of Buddhists and Jains, became targets of Islamic armies. Idols were broken up and damaged. Spires and pillars were torn down by the invading armies from Persia, Central Asia and resident Sultans. Temples were looted of their treasury and parts reused to build or to <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_non-Islamic_places_of_worship_into_mosques" title="Conversion of non-Islamic places of worship into mosques">convert the temples into mosques</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> During some periods, Muslim emperors such as Akbar encouraged arts, helped repair and protect Hindu temples. In other periods, the Sultans and emperors led a campaign of temple destruction as well as forbade repairs to damaged temples.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Richard Eaton has listed 80 campaigns of Hindu temple site destruction stretching over centuries, particularly from the 12th through the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The religious conflict and desecrations continued during the British colonial era.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The destruction of Hindu temple sites was comparatively less in the southern parts of India, such as in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>. Cave style Hindu temples that were carved inside a rock, hidden and rediscovered centuries later, such as the Kailasha Temple, have also survived. These are now UNESCO world heritage sites.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs_and_etiquette">Customs and etiquette</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Customs and etiquette">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE,_PURI,_ORISSA,_INDIA,_ASIA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE%2C_PURI%2C_ORISSA%2C_INDIA%2C_ASIA.jpg/220px-PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE%2C_PURI%2C_ORISSA%2C_INDIA%2C_ASIA.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE%2C_PURI%2C_ORISSA%2C_INDIA%2C_ASIA.jpg/330px-PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE%2C_PURI%2C_ORISSA%2C_INDIA%2C_ASIA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE%2C_PURI%2C_ORISSA%2C_INDIA%2C_ASIA.jpg/440px-PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE%2C_PURI%2C_ORISSA%2C_INDIA%2C_ASIA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PURI_JAGANATHA_TEMPLE,_PURI,_ORISSA,_INDIA,_ASIA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jagannath_Temple,_Puri" title="Jagannath Temple, Puri">Jagannath Temple</a> at <a href="/wiki/Puri" title="Puri">Puri</a>, one of <a href="/wiki/Char_Dham" title="Char Dham">Char Dham</a>: the four main spiritual centers of Hinduism.</div></div></div>\n<p>The customs and etiquette varies across India. Devotees in major temples may bring in <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)#Offerings" title="Puja (Hinduism)">symbolic offerings</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a>. This includes <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flower" title="Flower">flowers</a>, sweets and other symbols of the bounty of the natural world. Temples in India are usually surrounded with small shops selling these offerings.\n</p><p>When inside the temple, devotees keep both hands folded (<a href="/wiki/Namaste" title="Namaste">namaste</a> mudra). The inner sanctuary, where the <a href="/wiki/Murtis" class="mw-redirect" title="Murtis">murtis</a> reside, is known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Garbhagriha" title="Garbhagriha">garbhagriha</a></i>. It symbolizes the birthplace of the universe, the meeting place of the gods and mankind, and the threshold between the transcendental and the phenomenal worlds.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> It is in this inner shrine that devotees seek a darsana of, where they offer prayers. Devotees may or may not be able to personally present their offerings at the feet of the deity. In most large Indian temples, only the <a href="/wiki/Pujari" title="Pujari">pujaris</a> (priest) are allowed to enter into the main sanctum.<sup id="cite_ref-Narayanan_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Narayanan-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Temple management staff typically announce the hours of operation, including timings for special <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">pujas</a>. These timings and nature of special puja vary from temple to temple. Additionally, there may be specially allotted times for devotees to perform circumambulations (or <a href="/wiki/Pradakshina" class="mw-redirect" title="Pradakshina">pradakshina</a>) around the temple.<sup id="cite_ref-Narayanan_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Narayanan-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Visitors and worshipers to large Hindu temples may be required to deposit their shoes and other footwear before entering. Where this is expected, the temples provide an area and help staff to store footwear. Dress codes vary. It is customary in temples in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, for men to remove shirts and to cover pants and shorts with a traditional cloth known as a <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vasthiram&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vasthiram (page does not exist)">Vasthiram</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In Java and Bali (Indonesia), before one enters the most sacred parts of a Hindu temple, shirts are required as well as <a href="/wiki/Sarong" title="Sarong">Sarong</a> around one\'s waist.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> At many other locations, this formality is unnecessary.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variations_in_Hindu_temples">Regional variations in Hindu temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Regional variations in Hindu temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Indian_temples">North Indian temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: North Indian temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:440px;max-width:440px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:218px;max-width:218px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Kedarnath_Temple.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Kedarnath_Temple.jpg/216px-Kedarnath_Temple.jpg" width="216" height="144" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Kedarnath_Temple.jpg/324px-Kedarnath_Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Kedarnath_Temple.jpg/432px-Kedarnath_Temple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Kedarnath_Temple" title="Kedarnath Temple">Kedarnath Temple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:218px;max-width:218px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg/216px-Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg" width="216" height="144" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg/324px-Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg/432px-Shiv_Temple_-_Assam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Sivasagar_Sivadol" title="Sivasagar Sivadol">Sivasagar Sivadol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>North Indian temples are referred to as Nagara style of temple architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-adamhardy_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adamhardy-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> They have <i>sanctum sanctorum</i> where the deity is present, open on one side from where the devotee obtains <i><a href="/wiki/Dar%C5%9Bana" title="Dar\xc5\x9bana">dar\xc5\x9bana</a></i>. There may or may not be many more surrounding corridors, halls, etc. However, there will be space for devotees to go around the temple in clockwise fashion <a href="/wiki/Circumambulation" title="Circumambulation">circumambulation</a>. In North Indian temples, the tallest towers are built over the <i>sanctum sanctorum</i> in which the deity is installed.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The north India Nagara style of temple designs often deploy <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a>-theme, where smaller parts of the temple are themselves images or geometric re-arrangement of the large temple, a concept found in French and Russian architecture such as the <a href="/wiki/Matryoshka_doll" title="Matryoshka doll">matryoshka</a> principle. One difference is the scope and cardinality, where Hindu temple structures deploy this principle in every dimension with garbhgriya as the primary locus, and each pada as well as zones serving as additional centers of <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">loci</a>. This makes a Nagara Hindu temple architecture symbolically a perennial expression of movement and time, of centrifugal growth fused with the idea of unity in everything.<sup id="cite_ref-adamhardy_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adamhardy-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_West_Bengal">Temples in West Bengal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Temples in West Bengal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dakshineswar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Dakshineswar.jpg/200px-Dakshineswar.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Dakshineswar.jpg/300px-Dakshineswar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Dakshineswar.jpg/400px-Dakshineswar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dakshineswar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dakshineswar_Kali_Temple" title="Dakshineswar Kali Temple">Dakshineswar Kali Temple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_architecture#West_Bengal_terra_cotta_temple_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali architecture">Bengali terra cotta temple architecture</a> is found. Due to lack of suitable stone in the alluvial soil locally available, the temple makers had to resort to other materials instead of stone. This gave rise to using <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> as a medium for temple construction. Terracotta exteriors with rich carvings are a unique feature of Bengali temples. The town of <a href="/wiki/Bishnupur,_Bankura" title="Bishnupur, Bankura">Vishnupur</a> in West Bengal is renowned for this type of architecture. There is also a popular style of building known as <a href="/wiki/Navaratna_(architecture)" title="Navaratna (architecture)">Navaratna</a> (nine-towered) or Pancharatna (five-towered). An example of Navaratna style is the <a href="/wiki/Dakshineswar_Kali_Temple" title="Dakshineswar Kali Temple">Dakshineswar Kali Temple</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Odisha">Temples in Odisha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Temples in Odisha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg/200px-Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg/300px-Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg/400px-Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rajarani_Temple_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rajarani_Temple" title="Rajarani Temple">Rajarani Temple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhubaneswar" title="Bhubaneswar">Bhubaneswar</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a> temple architecture is known as <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_architecture" title="Kalinga architecture">Kalinga architecture</a>, classifies the spire into three parts, the <i>B\xc4\x81\xe1\xb8\x8da</i> (lower limb), the <i>Gan\xe1\xb8\x8di</i> (body) and the <i>Cu\xe1\xb8\xb7a/Mastaka</i> (head). Each part is decorated in a different manner. <a href="/wiki/Kalinga_architecture" title="Kalinga architecture">Kalinga architecture</a> is a style which flourished in Kalinga, the name for kingdom that included ancient Odisha. It includes three styles: <i>Rekha Deula</i>, <i>Pidha Deula</i> and <i>Khakhara Deula</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The former two are associated with <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surya" title="Surya">Surya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> temples while the third is mainly associated with <a href="/wiki/Chamunda" title="Chamunda">Chamunda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a> temples. The <i>Rekha Deula</i> and <i>Khakhara Deula</i> houses the <i><a href="/wiki/Sanctum_sanctorum" title="Sanctum sanctorum">sanctum sanctorum</a></i> while the <i>Pidha Deula</i> style includes space for outer dancing and offering halls.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_of_Goa_and_other_Konkani_temples">Temples of Goa and other Konkani temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Temples of Goa and other Konkani temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG/200px-Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG" width="200" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG/300px-Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG/400px-Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saptakoteshwar_Temple.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saptakoteshwar Temple, Goa.</div></div></div>\n<p>The temple architecture of Goa is quite unique. As Portuguese colonial hegemony increased, Goan Hindu temples became the rallying point to local resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>  Many these temples are not more than 500 years old, and are a unique blend of original Goan temple architecture, Dravidian, Nagar and <a href="/wiki/Hemadpanthi" class="mw-redirect" title="Hemadpanthi">Hemadpanthi</a> temple styles with some <a href="/wiki/British_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="British architecture">British</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Portuguese architecture">Portuguese architectural</a> influences. Goan temples were built using sedimentary rocks, wood, limestone and clay tiles, and copper sheets were used for the roofs. These temples were decorated with mural art called as <i><a href="/wiki/Kaavi_art" title="Kaavi art">Kavi kala</a></i> or <i>ocher art</i>. The interiors have murals and wood carvings depicting scenes from the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu mythology</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Indian_and_Sri_Lankan_temples">South Indian and Sri Lankan temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: South Indian and Sri Lankan temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:440px;max-width:440px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:218px;max-width:218px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Natarajartemple1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Natarajartemple1.jpg/216px-Natarajartemple1.jpg" width="216" height="144" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Natarajartemple1.jpg/324px-Natarajartemple1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Natarajartemple1.jpg/432px-Natarajartemple1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The gopuram (tower) of <a href="/wiki/Thillai_Nataraja_Temple,_Chidambaram" class="mw-redirect" title="Thillai Nataraja Temple, Chidambaram">Natarajar Temple</a>, a typical South Indian temple complex in <a href="/wiki/Chidambaram" title="Chidambaram">Chidambaram</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:218px;max-width:218px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Spiritual_16.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Spiritual_16.jpg/216px-Spiritual_16.jpg" width="216" height="144" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Spiritual_16.jpg/324px-Spiritual_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Spiritual_16.jpg/432px-Spiritual_16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3072" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Koneswaram_Temple" title="Koneswaram Temple">Koneswaram Temple</a>, a Tamil <a href="/wiki/Saivaite" class="mw-redirect" title="Saivaite">Saivate temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trincomalee" title="Trincomalee">Tirukonamalai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>South Indian temples have a large gopuram, a monumental tower, usually ornate, at the entrance of the temple. This forms a prominent feature of <a href="/wiki/Koil" title="Koil">Koils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple_architecture" title="Hindu temple architecture">Hindu temples</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_architecture" title="Dravidian architecture">Dravidian style</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ching2_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ching2-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> They are topped by the <i>kalasam</i>, a bulbous stone <a href="/wiki/Finial" title="Finial">finial</a>. They function as gateways through the walls that surround the temple complex.<sup id="cite_ref-ching_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ching-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The gopuram\'s origins can be traced back to early structures of the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamil people">Tamil</a> kings <a href="/wiki/Pallavas" class="mw-redirect" title="Pallavas">Pallavas</a>; and by the twelfth century, under the <a href="/wiki/Pandya" class="mw-redirect" title="Pandya">Pandya</a> rulers, these gateways became a dominant feature of a temple\'s outer appearance, eventually overshadowing the inner sanctuary which became obscured from view by the gopuram\'s colossal size.<sup id="cite_ref-mitchell_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mitchell-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> It also dominated the inner sanctum in amount of ornamentation. Often a shrine has more than one gopuram.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> They also appear in architecture outside India, especially <a href="/wiki/Khmer_architecture" title="Khmer architecture">Khmer architecture</a>, as at <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a>. A koil may have multiple gopurams, typically constructed into multiple walls in tiers around the main shrine. The temple\'s walls are typically square with the outer most wall having gopuras. The sanctum sanctorium and its towering roof (the central deity\'s shrine) are also called the <i><a href="/wiki/Vimana_(shrine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vimana (shrine)">vimanam</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The inner sanctum has restricted access with only priests allowed beyond a certain point.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Kerala">Temples in Kerala</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Temples in Kerala">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg/200px-Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg" width="200" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg/300px-Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg/400px-Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1282" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vadakkumnatha_Kshethram_-_Thekke_Nada.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vadakkunnathan_Temple" title="Vadakkunnathan Temple">Vadakkunnathan Temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thrissur" title="Thrissur">Thrissur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Temples in Kerala have a different architectural style (keeping the same essence of Vastu), especially due to climatic differences Kerala have with other parts of India with larger rainfall. The temple roof is mostly tiled and is sloped and the walls are often square, the innermost shrine being entirely enclosed in another four walls to which only the pujari (priest) enters. The walls are decorated with either mural paintings or rock sculptures which many times are emphasised on Dwarapalakas.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Temples_in_Karnataka_.28Tulu_Nadu.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Karnataka_(Tulu_Nadu)">Temples in Karnataka (Tulu Nadu)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Temples in Karnataka (Tulu Nadu)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Temples in Karnataka (Tulu Nadu) have a unique style of architecture and have been constructed according to vasthu. Among these Shri Agnidurga Gopalkrishna Temple (Gurupur, Mangalore), Sri Gokarnanatheshwara Temple (Kadri), Sri Kudtheri Mahamaya Temple, Sri Sowthedka Temple, Sri Mahothobaara Kotilingeshwara Temple, Sri Chandramouleswara Temple, Sri Brahma Baidarkala Garadi Temple, Sri Durga Parameshwari Temple, Sri Somanatheshwara Temple, Sri Manjunatha Temple, Sri Subramanya Temple, Sri Mahaganapati Temple, Sri Durga Parameshwari Temple, Sri Durga Parameshwari Temple, Sri Adi Janardana Temple, Sri Mookambika Temple, Sri Durgaparameshwari Temple, Sri Vinayaka Temple, Sri Shankara Narayana Temple, Sri Durga Parameshwari Temple, Sri Vishwanatha Temple, Sri Amrutheshwara Temple, Sri Subramanya Temple, Sri Raja Rajeshwari Temple, Sri Kadri Manjunatha Temple, Sri Mangaladevi Temple, Sri Mahalingeshwara Temple, Sri Somanatheshwara Temple, Sri Durga Parameshwari Temple, Sri Vishnu Murthy Temple, Sri Durga Parameshwari Temple, Sri Mahalingeshwara Mahaganapati Temple, Sri Janardana Swamy Temple, Sri Ananteshwara Vinayaka Temple, Sri Mahisha Mardini Temple among others are famous temples and with lot of religious activities.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Tamil_Nadu">Temples in Tamil Nadu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Temples in Tamil Nadu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Temple construction reached its peak during rule of <a href="/wiki/Pallavas" class="mw-redirect" title="Pallavas">Pallavas</a>. They built various temples around <a href="/wiki/Kancheepuram" class="mw-redirect" title="Kancheepuram">Kancheepuram</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Narasimhavarman_II" title="Narasimhavarman II">Narasimhavarman II</a> built the <a href="/wiki/Shore_Temple" title="Shore Temple">Shore Temple</a> in Mamallapuram, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pandyas" class="mw-redirect" title="Pandyas">Pandyas</a> rule created temples such as the <a href="/wiki/Meenakshi_Amman_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Meenakshi Amman Temple">Meenakshi Amman Temple</a> at Madurai and <a href="/wiki/Nellaiappar_Temple" title="Nellaiappar Temple">Nellaiappar Temple</a> at Tirunelveli.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESastri197018\xe2\x80\x93182_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESastri197018\xe2\x80\x93182-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The Cholas were prolific temple builders right from the times of the first medieval king <a href="/wiki/Vijayalaya_Chola" title="Vijayalaya Chola">Vijayalaya Chola</a>. The Chola temples include <a href="/wiki/Nataraja" title="Nataraja">Nataraja</a> temple at Chidambaram, the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Ranganathaswamy_Temple,_Srirangam" class="mw-redirect" title="Sri Ranganathaswamy Temple, Srirangam">Sri Ranganathaswami Temple</a> at Srirangam, the <a href="/wiki/Brihadeshvara_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Brihadeshvara Temple">Brihadeshvara Temple</a> of Thanjavur, <a href="/wiki/Brihadeshvara_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Brihadeshvara Temple">Brihadeshvara Temple</a> of Gangaikonda Cholapuram and the <a href="/wiki/Airavatesvara_Temple" title="Airavatesvara Temple">Airavatesvara Temple</a> of Darasuram which are among the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Sites">World Heritage Sites</a>. The Nayaks of Madurai reconstructed some of the well-known temples in Tamil Nadu such as the <a href="/wiki/Meenakshi_Temple" title="Meenakshi Temple">Meenakshi Temple</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-susanlchap4_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susanlchap4-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a href="/wiki/File:An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg" class="image"><img alt="aerial image of a temple campus." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg/800px-An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg" width="800" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg/1200px-An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg/1600px-An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4365" data-file-height="1430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:An_aerial_view_of_Madurai_city_from_atop_of_Meenakshi_Amman_temple.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An aerial view of the <a href="/wiki/Meenakshi_Temple" title="Meenakshi Temple">Meenakshi Temple</a> from the top of the southern <a href="/wiki/Gopuram" title="Gopuram">gopuram</a>, looking north. The temple was rebuilt by the <a href="/wiki/Vijayanagar_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Vijayanagar Empire">Vijayanagar Empire</a>.</div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Cambodia">Temples in Cambodia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Temples in Cambodia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bantay_srei.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Bantay_srei.jpg/220px-Bantay_srei.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Bantay_srei.jpg/330px-Bantay_srei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Bantay_srei.jpg/440px-Bantay_srei.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="409" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bantay_srei.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Art relief at the Hindu temple <a href="/wiki/Banteay_Srei" title="Banteay Srei">Banteay Srei</a> in Cambodia</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a> was built as a Hindu temple by King Suryavarman II in the early 12th century in Yasodharapura (Khmer, present-day <a href="/wiki/Angkor" title="Angkor">Angkor</a>), the capital of the Khmer Empire, as his state temple and eventual mausoleum. Breaking from the Shaiva tradition of previous kings, Angkor Wat was instead dedicated to Vishnu. The Spire in Khmer Hindu temple is called Giri (mountain) and symbolizes the residence of gods just like Meru does in Bali Hindu mythology and Ku (Guha) does in Burmese Hindu mythology.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Angkor Wat is just one of numerous Hindu temples in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, most of them in ruins. Hundreds of Hindu temples are scattered from Siem Reap to Sambor Prei Kuk in central Cambodian region.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Nepal">Temples in Nepal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Temples in Nepal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pashupatinath_Temple" title="Pashupatinath Temple">Pashupatinath temple</a> is one of the important temples of Hindu religion which is situated in <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>It is built in a pagoda style and is surrounded by hundreds of temples and buildings built by kings. The temples top is made from pure gold.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:808px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg" class="image" title="Pashupatinath Temple from the other bank of Bagmati river, Kathmandu, Nepal."><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg/800px-Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg" width="800" height="221" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg/1200px-Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg/1600px-Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3192" data-file-height="883" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pashupati_dec_20_2009.jpg" title="File:Pashupati dec 20 2009.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Pashupatinath_Temple" title="Pashupatinath Temple">Pashupatinath Temple</a> from the other bank of Bagmati river, <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.</div>\n</div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Vietnam">Temples in Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Temples in Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:My_Son.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/My_Son.jpg/200px-My_Son.jpg" width="200" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/My_Son.jpg/300px-My_Son.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/My_Son.jpg/400px-My_Son.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1628" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:My_Son.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Partially ruined <a href="/wiki/M%E1%BB%B9_S%C6%A1n" title="M\xe1\xbb\xb9 S\xc6\xa1n">M\xe1\xbb\xb9 S\xc6\xa1n</a> Hindu temple complex, Vietnam.</div></div></div>\n<p>There are a number of Hindu temple clusters along the coast of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, with some on UNESCO world heritage site list.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Examples include <a href="/wiki/M%E1%BB%B9_S%C6%A1n" title="M\xe1\xbb\xb9 S\xc6\xa1n">M\xe1\xbb\xb9 S\xc6\xa1n</a> \xe2\x80\x93 a cluster of 70 temples with earliest dated to be from the 4th century AD and dedicated to Siva, while others are dedicated to Hindu deities Krishna, Vishnu and others. These temples, internally and with respect to each other, are also built on the Hindu perfect square grid concept. Other sites in Vietnam with Hindu temples include Phan Rang with the Cham temple <a href="/wiki/Po_Klong_Garai" title="Po Klong Garai">Po Klong Garai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Indonesia">Temples in Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Temples in Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:440px;max-width:440px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:218px;max-width:218px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg/216px-Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg" width="216" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg/324px-Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg/432px-Prambanan_Complex_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3120" data-file-height="2382" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Candi <a href="/wiki/Prambanan" title="Prambanan">Prambanan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:218px;max-width:218px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG/216px-Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG" width="216" height="162" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG/324px-Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG/432px-Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan_3.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Pura_Ulun_Danu_Bratan" title="Pura Ulun Danu Bratan">Pura Ulun Danu Bratan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Ancient Hindu temples in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> are called <i><a href="/wiki/Candi_of_Indonesia" title="Candi of Indonesia">Candi</a></i> (read: <i>chandi</i>). Hindu temples of ancient <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> bear resemblances with temples of <a href="/wiki/South_Indian" class="mw-redirect" title="South Indian">South Indian</a> style. The largest of these is the 9th century Javanese Hindu temple, <a href="/wiki/Prambanan" title="Prambanan">Prambanan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>, now a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> world heritage site. It was designed as three concentric squares and has 224 temples. The inner square contains 16 temples dedicated to major Hindu deities, of which <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> temple is the largest. The temple has extensive wall reliefs and carvings illustrating the stories from the Hindu epic <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balinese_Hinduism" title="Balinese Hinduism">Hindu</a> temple is known as "<a href="/wiki/Pura_(Balinese_temple)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pura (Balinese temple)">Pura</a>", which is designed as an open-air worship place in a walled compound. The compound walls have a series of intricately decorated gates without doors for the devotee to enter. The design, plan and layout of the holy pura follows a square layout.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_in_Thailand">Temples in Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Temples in Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg/200px-Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg/300px-Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg/400px-Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5568" data-file-height="3712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wat_Khaek_Silom_Sri_Mariamman_Hindu_temple_Bangkok.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sri_Mariamman_Temple,_Bangkok" class="mw-redirect" title="Sri Mariamman Temple, Bangkok">Sri Mariamman Temple, Bangkok</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> has many notable Hindu temples including: the <a href="/wiki/Mariamman_Temple,_Bangkok" title="Mariamman Temple, Bangkok">Sri Mariammam temple</a> in Bangkok, the <a href="/wiki/Devasathan" title="Devasathan">Devasathan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Erawan_Shrine" title="Erawan Shrine">Erawan Shrine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prasat_Muang_Tam" title="Prasat Muang Tam">Prasat Muang Tam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sdok_Kok_Thom" title="Sdok Kok Thom">Sdok Kok Thom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phanom_Rung_Historical_Park" title="Phanom Rung Historical Park">Phanom Rung</a>. Most of the newer Hindu temples are of South Indian origin and were built by Tamil migrant communities. However, Thailand has many historic indigenous Hindu temples such as <a href="/wiki/Phanom_Rung_Historical_Park" title="Phanom Rung Historical Park">Phanom Rung</a>. Although most indigenous Hindu temples are ruins, a few such as <a href="/wiki/Devasathan" title="Devasathan">Devasathan</a> in Bangkok are actively used.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_outside_Asia">Temples outside Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Temples outside Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Hindu temples outside Asia</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse;text-align:center;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;margin:auto;width:480px"><tbody><tr States</a>.</div></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Many members of the South Asian diaspora have established Hindu mandirs outside India as a means of preserving and celebrating cultural and spiritual heritage abroad. Describing the hundreds of mandirs that can be found throughout the United States, scholar Gail M. Harley observes, "The temples serve as central locations where Hindus can come together to worship during holy festivals and socialize with other Hindus. Temples in America reflect the colorful kaleidoscopic aspects contained in Hinduism while unifying people who are disbursed throughout the American landscape."<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Numerous mandirs in North America and Europe have gained particular prominence and acclaim, many of which were built by the <a href="/wiki/Bochasanwasi_Akshar_Purushottam_Swaminarayan_Sanstha" title="Bochasanwasi Akshar Purushottam Swaminarayan Sanstha">Bochasanwasi Akshar Purushottam Swaminarayan Sanstha</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Temple_Society_of_North_America" title="Hindu Temple Society of North America">Hindu Temple Society of North America</a>, representing Sri Maha Vallabha <a href="/wiki/Ganapati" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganapati">Ganapati</a> <a href="/wiki/Devasthanam" title="Devasthanam">Devasthanam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: \xe0\xa4\xb6\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0\xe0\xa5\x80 \xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xb9\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xb5\xe0\xa4\xb2\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb2\xe0\xa4\xad \xe0\xa4\x97\xe0\xa4\xa3\xe0\xa4\xaa\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa4\xbf \xe0\xa4\xa6\xe0\xa5\x87\xe0\xa4\xb5\xe0\xa4\xb8\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xa5\xe0\xa4\xbe\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa5\x8d, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Americans" title="Tamil Americans">Tamil</a>: \xe0\xae\xb8\xe0\xaf\x8d\xe0\xae\xb0\xe0\xaf\x80 \xe0\xae\xae\xe0\xae\x95\xe0\xae\xbe \xe0\xae\xb5\xe0\xae\xb2\xe0\xaf\x8d\xe0\xae\xb2\xe0\xae\xaa \xe0\xae\x95\xe0\xae\xa3\xe0\xae\xaa\xe0\xae\xa4\xe0\xae\xbf \xe0\xae\xa4\xe0\xaf\x87\xe0\xae\xb5\xe0\xae\xb8\xe0\xaf\x8d\xe0\xae\xa4\xe0\xae\xbe\xe0\xae\xa9\xe0\xae\xae\xe0\xaf\x8d), in <a href="/wiki/Flushing,_Queens" title="Flushing, Queens">Flushing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indians_in_the_New_York_City_metropolitan_region" title="Indians in the New York City metropolitan region">New York City</a>, is the oldest Hindu temple in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>, and its <a href="/wiki/Cafeteria" title="Cafeteria">canteen</a> feeds 4,000 people a week, with as many as 10,000 during the <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a> (Deepavali) holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-FlushingHinduTempleCanteen_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlushingHinduTempleCanteen-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Temple_management">Temple management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Temple management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Survey_of_India" title="Archaeological Survey of India">Archaeological Survey of India</a> has control of most ancient temples of archaeological importance in India. In India, day-to-day activities of a temple is managed by a temple board <a href="/wiki/Committee" title="Committee">committee</a> that administers its <a href="/wiki/Finance" title="Finance">finances</a>, management, and events. Since independence, the autonomy of individual Hindu religious denominations to manage their own affairs with respect to temples of their own denomination has been severely eroded and the state governments have taken control of major Hindu temples in some countries; however, in others, such as the United States, private temple management autonomy has been preserved.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_nomenclature">Etymology and nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hindu_temple&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Etymology and nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></div></div><div style="position:absolute;top:44.053%;left:27.401%"><div style="position:absolute;left:-4px;top:-4px;line-height:0"><img alt="Hindu temple" Orange markers are UNESCO world heritage sites.</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, the liturgical language of Hinduism, the word <i>mandira</i> means "house" (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\xa8\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xa6\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xb0</span>). Ancient Sanskrit texts use many words for temple, such as <i>matha, vayuna, kirti, kesapaksha, devavasatha, vihara, suravasa, surakula, devatayatana, amaragara, devakula, devagrha, devabhavana, devakulika</i>, and <i>niketana</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Regionally, they are also known as <i>prasada</i>, <i>vimana</i>, <i>kshetra</i>, <i>gudi</i>, <i>ambalam</i>, <i>punyakshetram</i>, <i>deval</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Deula" title="Deula">deula</a></i>, <i>devasthanam</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Koil" title="Koil">kovil</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Candi_of_Indonesia" title="Candi of Indonesia">candi</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Balinese_temple" title="Balinese temple">pura</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Wat" title="Wat">wat</a></i>.\n</p><p>The following are the other names by which a Hindu temple is referred to in India:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Devasthana</i> (\xe0\xb2\xa6\xe0\xb3\x87\xe0\xb2\xb5\xe0\xb2\xb8\xe0\xb3\x8d\xe0\xb2\xa5\xe0\xb2\xbe\xe0\xb2\xa8) in <a href="/wiki/Kannada" title="Kannada">Kannada</a></li>\n<li><i>Deul</i>/<i>Doul</i>/<i>Dewaaloy</i> in <a href="/wiki/Assamese_language" title="Assamese language">Assamese</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Deul" title="Deul">Deval</a>/Raul/Mandir(\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\x82\xe0\xa4\xa6\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xb0)</i> in <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi</a></li>\n<li><i>Devro/Mindar</i> in <a href="/wiki/Rajasthani_language" title="Rajasthani language">Rajasthani</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Deula" title="Deula">Deula</a> (\xe0\xac\xa6\xe0\xad\x87\xe0\xac\x89\xe0\xac\xb3)/Mandira(\xe0\xac\xae\xe0\xac\xa6\xe0\xac\xbf\xe0\xac\xb0)</i> in <a href="/wiki/Odia_language" title="Odia language">Odia</a> and <i>Gudi</i> in Kosali Odia</li>\n<li><i>Gudi</i> (\xe0\xb0\x97\xe0\xb1\x81\xe0\xb0\xa1\xe0\xb0\xbf), <i>Devalayam</i> (\xe0\xb0\xa6\xe0\xb1\x87\xe0\xb0\xb5\xe0\xb0\xbe\xe0\xb0\xb2\xe0\xb0\xaf\xe0\xb0\x82), <i>Devasthanam</i> (\xe0\xb0\xa6\xe0\xb1\x87\xe0\xb0\xb5\xe0\xb0\xb8\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xa5\xe0\xb0\xbe\xe0\xb0\xa8\xe0\xb0\xae\xe0\xb1\x81), <i>Kovela</i> (\xe0\xb0\x95\xe0\xb1\x8b\xe0\xb0\xb5\xe0\xb1\x86\xe0\xb0\xb2), <i>Kshetralayam (\xe0\xb0\x95\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xb7\xe0\xb1\x87\xe0\xb0\xa4\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xb0\xe0\xb0\xbe\xe0\xb0\xb2\xe0\xb0\xaf\xe0\xb0\x82), </i>Punyakshetram<i> (\xe0\xb0\xaa\xe0\xb1\x81\xe0\xb0\xa3\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xaf\xe0\xb0\x95\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xb7\xe0\xb1\x87\xe0\xb0\xa4\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xb0\xe0\xb0\x82), or </i>Punyakshetralayam<i> (\xe0\xb0\xaa\xe0\xb1\x81\xe0\xb0\xa3\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xaf\xe0\xb0\x95\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xb7\xe0\xb1\x87\xe0\xb0\xa4\xe0\xb1\x8d\xe0\xb0\xb0\xe0\xb0\xbe\xe0\xb0\xb2\xe0\xb0\xaf\xe0\xb0\x82), </i>Mandiramu<i> (\xe0\xb0\xae\xe0\xb0\x82\xe0\xb0\xa6\xe0\xb0\xbf\xe0\xb0\xb0\xe0\xb0\xae\xe0\xb1\x81) in <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a></i></li></ul>\n<p><sup class="&quot;noprint" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style#Punctuation" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style"><span title="Quotation marks may be incomplete, unbalanced or improperly applied.">check quotation syntax</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Kovil" class="mw-redirect" title="Kovil">Kovil</a></i> or k\xc5\x8d-vill (\xe0\xae\x95\xe0\xaf\x8b\xe0\xae\xb5\xe0\xae\xbf\xe0\xae\xb2\xe0\xaf\x8d) and occasionally <i>Aalayam</i> (\xe0\xae\x86\xe0\xae\xb2\xe0\xae\xaf\xe0\xae\xae\xe0\xaf\x8d) in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a>; the Tamil word <i>Kovil</i> means "residence of God"<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i>Kshetram</i> (\xe0\xb4\x95\xe0\xb5\x8d\xe0\xb4\xb7\xe0\xb5\x87\xe0\xb4\xa4\xe0\xb5\x8d\xe0\xb4\xb0\xe0\xb4\x82), <i>Ambalam</i> (\xe0\xb4\x85\xe0\xb4\xae\xe0\xb5\x8d\xe0\xb4\xaa\xe0\xb4\xb2\xe0\xb4\x82), or <i>Kovil</i> (\xe0\xb4\x95\xe0\xb5\x8b\xe0\xb4\xb5\xe0\xb4\xbf\xe0\xb5\xbd) in <a href="/wiki/Malayalam" title="Malayalam">Malayalam</a></li>\n<li><i>Mandir</i> (\xe0\xa4\xae\xe0\xa4\x82\xe0\xa4\xa6\xe0\xa4\xbf\xe0\xa4\xb0) in <a href="/wiki/Hindi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">Marathi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> (\xe0\xa8\xae\xe0\xa9\xb0\xe0\xa8\xa6\xe0\xa8\xb0), <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a> (\xe0\xaa\xae\xe0\xaa\x82\xe0\xaa\xa6\xe0\xaa\xbf\xe0\xaa\xb0), and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> (\xd9\x85\xd9\x86\xd8\xaf\xd8\xb1)<sup id="cite_ref-Shackle1990_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shackle1990-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><i>Mondir</i> (\xe0\xa6\xae\xe0\xa6\xa8\xe0\xa7\x8d\xe0\xa6\xa6\xe0\xa6\xbf\xe0\xa6\xb0) in <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a></li></ul>\n<p>In Southeast Asia temples known as:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Candi_of_Indonesia" title="Candi of Indonesia">Candi</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">Javanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>, used both for Hindu or Buddhist temples.</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Balinese_temple" title="Balinese temple">Pura</a></i> in Hindu majority island of <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>, Indonesia.</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Wat" title="Wat">Wat</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, also applied to both Hindu and Buddhist temples.</li></ul>\n<dl><dt>Temple sites</dt></dl>\n<p>Some lands, including Varanasi, Puri, Kanchipuram, Dwarka, Amarnath, Kedarnath, Somnath, Mathura and Rameswara, are considered holy in Hinduism. They are called <i>k\xe1\xb9\xa3\xc3\xa9tra</i> (Sanskrit: \xe0\xa4\x95\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb7\xe0\xa5\x87\xe0\xa4\xa4\xe0\xa5\x8d\xe0\xa4\xb0<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>). A k\xe1\xb9\xa3\xc3\xa9tra has many temples, including one or more major ones. These temples and its location attracts pilgrimage called tirtha (or tirthayatra).<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:808px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a King (ed.), <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0710202345" title="Special:BookSources/978-0710202345">978-0710202345</a>, Routledge, pp. 68-69.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The square is symbolic and has Vedic origins from fire altar, <a href="/wiki/Agni" title="Agni">Agni</a>. The alignment along cardinal direction, similarly is an extension of Vedic rituals of three fires. This symbolism is also found among Greek and other ancient civilizations, through the <a href="/wiki/Gnomon" title="Gnomon">gnomon</a>. In Hindu temple manuals, design plans are described with 1, 4, 9, 16, 25, 36, 49, 64, 81 up to 1024 squares; 1 pada is considered the simplest plan, as a seat for a hermit or devotee to sit and meditate on, or make offerings with Vedic fire in front. The second design of 4 padas lacks the central core, and is also a meditative constructive. The 9 pada design has a sacred surrounded center, and is the template for the smallest temple. Older Hindu temple vastumandalas may use the 9 through 49 pada series, but 64 is considered the most sacred geometric grid in Hindu temples. It is also called Manduka, Bhekapada or Ajira in various ancient Sanskrit texts.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In addition to square (4) sided layout, Brhat Samhita also describes Vastu and mandala design principles based on a perfect triangle (3), hexagon (6), octagon (8) and hexadecagon (16) sided layouts, according to Stella Kramrisch.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rian et al. They also acted as great residential institutions and offered facilities for the study and stay of hundreds of students and teachers from far and near. Some of these were triple institutions, a college, a hostel and a hospital knit together. Many of such institutions are found described in the Chola inscriptions. (...) But the earliest known Vedic institution of advanced studies that existed in the Tamil country was the vidyasthana at Bahur near Pondicherry. A copper plate grant issued during the reign of Nripatungavarman (AD 877) records the gift of certain villages as <i>vidya-bhoga</i> for its maintenance."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-scharfetempleschools-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-scharfetempleschools_92-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-scharfetempleschools_92-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Hartmut Scharfe (2002), <i>From Temple schools to Universities</i>, in Education in Ancient India: Handbook of Oriental Studies, Brill Academic, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-9004125568" title="Special:BookSources/978-9004125568">978-9004125568</a>, pages 173-174</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-scharfetempleschools2-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-scharfetempleschools2_93-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-scharfetempleschools2_93-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Hartmut Scharfe (2002), <i>From Temple schools to Universities</i>, in Education in Ancient India: Handbook of Oriental Studies, Brill Academic, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-9004125568" title="Special:BookSources/978-9004125568">978-9004125568</a>, page 176-182</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Zysk1998p45-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Zysk1998p45_94-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Zysk1998p45_94-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Zysk1998p45_94-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Kenneth G.
